        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.0c14611"><meta property="og:title" content="Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/acsami.0c14611.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium (Na)-ion capacitors possess higher energy density than supercapacitors and higher power density than Na-ion batteries. However, kinetic mismatches between fast capacitive charge storage on the cathode and sluggish battery-type reactions on the anode lead to a poor charge/discharge rate capability and insufficient power output of Na-ion capacitors. Thus, developing suitable anode materials for Na-ion capacitors is urgently desirable. This work demonstrates an electrochemically exfoliated graphite (EEG) anode with enhanced capacitive charge storage, greatly boosting the Na-ion reaction kinetics of co-intercalation. The EEG anode shows a high reversible capacity of 109 mAh g–1 and maintains a good capacity retention of 90% after 1000 cycles. The assembled Na-ion capacitor using the EEG anode can finish the charge/discharge process in less than 10 s, which achieves an ultrahigh power density of 17,500 W kg–1 with an energy density of 17 Wh kg–1. The high capacitive contributions at both the anode and cathode contribute to the fast rate capability and high power output of the fabricated Na-ion capacitors. This work will promote the development of ultrafast charging sodium-ion storage devices."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/acsami.0c14611.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da86f90f77b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da86f90f77b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'a4016c5c-597d-4a9d-8ff7-003b04358ed0'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da86f90f77b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Boosting Capacitive Sodium-Ion Storage in Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite for Sodium-Ion Capacitors", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.0c14611", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Yuping++Wu" data-id="article_author_search">Yuping Wu</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/medium/am0c14611_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14611&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium (Na)-ion capacitors possess higher energy density than supercapacitors and higher power density than Na-ion batteries. However, kinetic mismatches between fast capacitive charge storage on the cathode and sluggish battery-type reactions on the anode lead to a poor charge/discharge rate capability and insufficient power output of Na-ion capacitors. Thus, developing suitable anode materials for Na-ion capacitors is urgently desirable. This work demonstrates an electrochemically exfoliated graphite (EEG) anode with enhanced capacitive charge storage, greatly boosting the Na-ion reaction kinetics of co-intercalation. The EEG anode shows a high reversible capacity of 109 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and maintains a good capacity retention of 90% after 1000 cycles. The assembled Na-ion capacitor using the EEG anode can finish the charge/discharge process in less than 10 s, which achieves an ultrahigh power density of 17,500 W kg<sup>–1</sup> with an energy density of 17 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. The high capacitive contributions at both the anode and cathode contribute to the fast rate capability and high power output of the fabricated Na-ion capacitors. This work will promote the development of ultrafast charging sodium-ion storage devices.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D52635%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTing%2BHuang%252C%2BZaichun%2BLiu%252C%2BFeng%2BYu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D47%26contentID%3Dacsami.0c14611%26title%3DBoosting%2BCapacitive%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorage%2Bin%2BElectrochemically%2BExfoliated%2BGraphite%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BCapacitors%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D52642%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Anode%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290916&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Capacitors" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Capacitors" class="article__tags__link">Capacitors</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon" class="article__tags__link">Carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrochemically+Exfoliated+Graphite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrochemically%20exfoliated%20graphite">electrochemically exfoliated graphite</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Capacitive+Sodium-ion+Storage&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_capacitive%20sodium-ion%20storage">capacitive sodium-ion storage</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Solvent+Co-intercalation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_solvent%20co-intercalation">solvent co-intercalation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Capacitors&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20capacitors">sodium-ion capacitors</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=First-principles+Calculations&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_first-principles%20calculations">first-principles calculations</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The combination of rechargeable batteries and supercapacitors led to the emergence and development of metal ion (Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, <i>etc</i>.) hybrid capacitors. Currently, Li-ion capacitors are the most prominent hybrid ion capacitors. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> As the demand for Li resources continues to grow, there are widespread concerns about the cost and sustainability of Li-ion capacitors owing to the scarcity of Li resources on the earth’s crust. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> As promising alternative systems, Na-ion hybrid capacitors are attracting great interest for powering consumer electronics to electric vehicles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 5 to 15">(5−15)</a> Typically, Na-ion capacitors consist of battery-type anodes and capacitor-type cathodes in Na-salt containing electrolytes. They operate via a hybrid energy storage mechanism with diffusion-controlled battery reactions at anodes and surface-controlled capacitive processes at cathodes, which offer a much higher energy density than supercapacitors and a much higher power density than Na-ion batteries. To date, various anode materials have been reported for Na-ion capacitors, including disordered carbon materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref7 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref7 ref9" aria-label="reference 5, 7 and 9">(5,7,9)</a> metal oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 11, 15 and 16">(11,15,16)</a> transition-metal sulfides/selenides/carbides/phosphides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref11 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref11 ref14" aria-label="reference 8, 11 and 14">(8,11,14)</a><i>etc.</i> However, Na-ion capacitors have a significant challenge about the kinetic imbalance between the sluggish faradic redox reactions at anodes and fast non-faradic adsorption/desorption processes at cathodes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 10 and 12 to 17">(10,12−17)</a> Specifically, compared with capacitor-type cathodes, the faradaic battery-type anodes have sluggish diffusion-controlled reaction mechanisms and a large volume variation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18−21)</a> leading to limited power density and cycle lifespan. Moreover, most anode materials for Na-ion capacitors are semiconducting and have low electrical conductivity, which greatly impedes the improvement of electrochemical performance. In general, further development of Na-ion capacitors is seriously hindered by the lack of suitable anode materials. Therefore, exploring highly conductive anode materials with fast capacitive/or pseudocapacitive charge storage will be a key to address the kinetic imbalance issues of anodes and cathodes in Na-ion capacitors.</div><div class="NLM_p">As a conductive anode material, graphite has been widely used in commercial Li-ion batteries for decades due to its low cost, high stability, robust structure, and environmental friendliness. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Despite its high reversibility for Li-ion storage, graphite fails to be a promising anode material for Na-ion storage because Na ions hardly intercalate into graphite layers in standard carbonate-based electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Fortunately, the use of ether-based electrolytes enables reversible Na-ion storage in graphite due to the co-intercalation of Na-ion and ether-based solvent molecules with the formation of “ternary graphite intercalation compounds”. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 24 to 29">(24−29)</a> In ether-based diglyme electrolytes, graphite as an anode for Na-ion storage shows several redox peaks in a potential range of 0.5–1.5 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> with specific capacities of approximately 80–150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The theoretical simulation indicates that the diglyme–graphite interaction boosts the interlayer coupling strength in Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme co-intercalated graphite, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> thereby improving the resistance of graphite against exfoliation. In addition, diglyme electrolytes can form a thin, uniform, and efficient electrode–electrolyte interphase even on anode materials with an ultrahigh specific surface area, thereby leading to low irreversible capacity loss and good stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 31 to 33">(31−33)</a> Although graphite can be used successfully in Na-ion batteries, it is still not suitable as an anode material for high-performance Na-ion capacitors because of the sluggish reaction kinetic and the large volume expansion ratio (up to 247%) due to the Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme co-intercalation.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, electrochemically exfoliated graphite (EEG) was developed as the anode for high-performance Na-ion capacitors. The EEG electrode exhibits broader redox peaks and more sloping charge/discharge profiles with a less discharge plateau than the graphite electrode. Compared with the graphite electrode, the 2D ultrathin nanosheets of EEG can enhance the reaction kinetic of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in a diglyme-based electrolyte. Moreover, the well-stacked EEG film anode with open void can alleviate the volume expansion issues of Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme co-intercalation. Thus, the EEG anode in the diglyme electrolyte exhibits better cycle stability than the pristine graphite anode. The fabricated Na-ion capacitor based on the EEG anode can be charged/discharged within a few seconds and achieve a high power density of 17,500 W kg<sup>–1</sup>, outperforming those of Na-ion capacitors based on battery-typed anodes or semiconducting pseudocapacitive anodes.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis of Electrochemically Exfoliated Graphite</h3><div class="NLM_p last">EEG was prepared by exfoliating a graphite foil using an electrochemical method with minor modifications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> A two-electrode cell was fabricated using two graphite foils as both the anode and cathode in aqueous 0.1 M NH<sub>4</sub>NO<sub>3</sub> solution. Direct current voltages of 10 V were applied to the two-electrode device, and positive/negative poles exchanged periodically until all the graphite foils were exfoliated into the solution. The EEG powder was collected through vacuum filtration and was washed several times with deionized water and ethanol to remove residual NH<sub>4</sub>NO<sub>3</sub>. Finally, the EEG powder for the subsequent material characterization was obtained through a freeze drying method. After checking the mass difference of the graphite foil before and after the electrochemical exfoliation, the yield of the EEG powder was calculated to be in the range of 16–31% based on different exfoliated time (24–48 h).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The EEG film was prepared using a vacuum filtration method when the resulting EEG powder prior to freeze drying was dispersed in <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide through sonication. The morphologies of the obtained EEG powders and films were evaluated using a field emission scanning electron microscope (Zeiss Gemini 500) and a transmission electron microscope (JEOL, JEM-2010, Japan). Raman spectra of the EEG powder were obtained using a Raman spectrometer (Ntegra Aura/Spectra) from NT-MDT (Moscow, Russia) using an HeNe laser (532 nm) with a 2 cm<sup>–1</sup> resolution.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The free-standing EEG electrode was obtained by cutting the above EEG film. Half cells were constructed using coin-type 2032 cells with Na metal as the counter and reference electrodes. A glass fiber membrane and 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether were used as the separator and the electrolyte, respectively. AC cathodes were prepared by mixing commercial AC, a polyvinylidene fluoride binder, and conductive carbon black agent with a mass ratio of 8:1:1 in an <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone solvent. The above slurry was then cast on an Al foil current collector and dried at 120 °C for 12 h in a vacuum oven. The areal mass loadings of active EEG and AC electrodes were ∼0.8 and 1.3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>, respectively. The Na-ion capacitors were fabricated using the EEG electrode as the anode and the AC electrode as the cathode in the same electrolyte. All half and full cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glove box with both oxygen and water contents below 0.1 ppm. Cyclic voltammograms and electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were collected using an electrochemical workstation (CHI660E) and a multichannel battery testing system (LAND CT2001A).</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Theoretical Calculation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">DFT-based first-principles calculations reported herein were performed with a CASTEP package <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> within the generalized gradient approximation (GGA), as formulated by the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) functional. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The final set of energies for all calculations was computed with an energy cutoff of 598.7 eV. All atoms were fully relaxed until the convergence criteria for energy set at 10<sup>–5</sup> eV were reached, and the residual forces on each atom became smaller than 0.02 eVÅ<sup>–1</sup>. The Brillouin zone integration was performed with 1 × 1 × 1 Γ-centered Monkhorst–Pack k-point meshes in geometry optimization calculations for graphene and solvated Na ion and 2 × 2 × 2 for layer graphene. Migration barrier calculations were performed with the linear synchronous transit/quadratic synchronous transit (LST/QST) method <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> in a large 5 × 5 × 1 supercell.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of EEG was characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). A sheet-like structure with a highly wrinkled surface can be observed from the SEM image of EEG (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) image of the EEG nanosheets shows an interlayer spacing of ∼0.33 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), corresponding to the (002) planes of graphites. HR-TEM also indicates that the layer numbers of EEG after electrochemical exfoliation are approximately 10–15 layers. Moreover, as shown in the XRD patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>), the intensity of the (002) peak of EEG is weakened compared to that of pristine graphite, indicating less number of graphene layers in EEG. In addition, EEG exhibits Raman peaks at 1350, 1580, and 2700 cm<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), which are assigned to the D band (sp<sup>3</sup> defects originating from disordered carbon), G band (sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized graphitic carbon) and symmetric 2D band, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 34, 38 and 39">(34,38,39)</a> The intensity ratio of D versus G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) was much lower than conventional reduced graphene oxides, implying a high content of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon domains and low content of defects in EEG. The nitrogen adsorption–desorption tests show a Brunauer–Emmett–Teller surface area of 63 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> for the EEG sample (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/medium/am0c14611_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of raw graphite and prepared EEG.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14611&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of graphite before electrochemical exfoliation and after electrochemical exfoliation were tested in coin cells using the ether-based electrolyte (1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve of the graphite electrode before electrochemical exfoliation. Several reduction peaks reveal that the graphite electrode experiences multistages of Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme co-insertion during the cathodic process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref40" aria-label="reference 24 to 28 and 40">(24−28,40)</a> Differently, the redox peaks for the EEG electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) are much broader than those for the graphite electrode. Furthermore, the peak separation of one pair of the main redox peak in the EEG electrode (0.21 V) is much smaller than that in the pristine graphite electrode (0.62 V), suggesting lower polarization in the EEG electrode. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a> displays the galvanostatic discharge/charge of the graphite electrode in the first cycle at a current density of 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The graphite electrode displays discharge and charge capacities of 140/120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a coulombic efficiency of 86%. The initial irreversible capacity loss can be mainly ascribed to a small amount of irreversible electrolyte decomposition accompanied by the formation of the electrode–electrolyte interphase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Then, the coulombic efficiency quickly rises to 99% in the next cycles. This is very common for anode materials when the voltage range is below 1 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, which is also observed in the EEG anode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>). The initially irreversible electrolyte decomposition may lead to a slightly increased charge transfer resistance of the EEG electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). After the initial cycles, the discharge voltage profiles of the pristine graphite electrode shows two different regions with a flat plateau at 0.7 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> and a slopping plateau at a potential range of 0.7–0.1 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). Regarding the EEG electrode, the capacity from the sloping region greatly increases and that from a flat plateau decreases (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d), which are basically consistent with the result of CV curves. Although the flat plateau region decreases, the specific capacity of the EEG electrode (109 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) is only slightly lower than that of the pristine graphite electrode (120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) because of the enhanced sloping plateau region in the EEG electrode. Notably, the EEG electrode shows better cycling stability than the graphite electrode. For the pristine graphite electrode, the specific capacity decreases to 95 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles with a 79% capacity retention (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). However, the capacity retention of the EEG electrode is up to 90% after 1000 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f). The difference of cycling performance indicates that the well-stacked EEG film electrode with open voids (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) can alleviate the large volume changes due to the co-intercalation of solvent molecules.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/medium/am0c14611_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Comparison of electrochemical properties of graphite before electrochemical exfoliation and after electrochemical exfoliation. (a) CV curve of the pristine graphite electrode and (b) CV curve of the EEG electrode at 1.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles of (c) pristine graphite and (d) EEG electrodes at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup> after the initial cycle. The cycle stability of (e) pristine graphite and (f) EEG electrodes at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The insets are the corresponding galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles at 500th and 1000th cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14611&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition, both the redox peak currents of the EEG electrode gradually increased with sweep rates rising from 0.5 to 2.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>) Typically, the anodic and cathodic peak current (<i>i</i>) increases with the scan rate (<i>v</i>) in accordance with the power law of <i>i</i> = <i>a</i> × <i>v<sup>b</sup></i>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters. The <i>b</i> values close to 0.5 and 1 indicate diffusion-limited reaction and surface-limited charge storage processes, respectively. For the EEG anode, the obtained <i>b</i> values for reduction and oxidation peaks were 0.89 and 0.71 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), respectively, indicating the possible existence of partial capacitive charge storage in the EEG electrode. The contributions from capacitive processes can be estimated using <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + k<sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> represents the surface-controlled capacitive contribution, whereas <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> stands for the diffusion-controlled intercalation contribution. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 41 to 44">(41−44)</a> The estimated capacitive contributions in the EEG electrode are ∼65 and ∼81% even at small sweep rates of 0.5 and 1.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). There are still several redox peaks in the identified capacitive contribution. This reveals that EEG has possibly a partial charge storage from the pseudocapacitive Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme co-intercalation process. It is common that a 2D ultrathin nanosheet can enhance capacitive behaviors for Li- or Na-ion storage in non-aqueous electrolytes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 45 to 47">(45−47)</a> because pseudocapacitive behavior is a surface/near-surface process. Notably, the above separation of the total current/charge into the faradaic part and non-faradaic part cannot reveal the precise contribution ratio and it is just a rough estimate of the pseudocapacitive contribution in the EEG electrode. The main reason is that the usage of isopotential plots of the scan rate-dependent current density for the above separation calculation cannot properly take into account scan rate-dependent changes of the peak potentials and widths. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> To determine the contributions of the non-faradaic, pseudocapacitive, and diffusion-controlled capacities, it is necessary to explore a more precise characterization methodology that can directly probe a surface charge storage process in the future. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To gain profound insights into the solvated Na-ion storage behaviors in the EEG electrode, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to investigate the structure of a solvated Na ion by diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME) molecules. The optimized structure of the free Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME complex shows that the solvated Na ion is threefold coordinated with O-atoms of DEGDME molecules (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). The Na-ion storage mechanism in the EEG electrode mainly involves adsorption/desorption at planar surfaces and intercalation into the graphite interlayer. Thus, we calculated the energy barriers for the diffusion of DEGDME solvated Na ions from the site determined by atomic relaxation to the adjacent site on the graphite surface and in the graphite interlayer (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The related calculations were carried out using the LST/QST method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50−52)</a> The energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDEM diffusion on the graphite surface is only 0.21 eV, while the energy barrier in the graphite interlayer is 0.59 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). The lower energy barrier means a faster diffusion. The fast Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME diffusivity on graphite surface can be attributed to the DEGDME–graphene vdW interaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Specifically, the DEGDME–graphene vdW interaction would make a very flat potential energy surface for Na-ion diffusion. The DEGDME molecules surrounding one Na ion moves in a direction horizontal to the graphene surface, and the Na ion is in the middle of the sandwich structure of “DEGDME-Na-graphene” (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). However, for the Na-ion diffusion in the interlayer of graphite, the Na-ion position in Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME complexes is slightly distorted; one of the methoxy groups (−OCH<sub>3</sub>) locates in the direction of Na-ion diffusion (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). This will lead to a steric hindrance when Na ion moves in the interlayer space of graphite, thereby causing a relatively high diffusion barrier and slow diffusion rate in graphite. In addition, the energy barrier of the desolvation step of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDEM (0.67 eV) is much higher than that of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDEM co-intercalation in graphite layers, indicating possible Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME co-intercalation in graphite layers without desolvation.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/medium/am0c14611_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Theoretical calculations of the structure of the Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME complex and its diffusion on the graphite surface and in the graphite interlayer. (a) Optimized structure of the Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME complex. (b) Optimized energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME desolvation and Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDEM diffusion on the graphite surface and in the graphite interlayer. The optimized structures of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME (c) on the graphite surface and (d) in the graphite interlayer. The corresponding adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup>-DEGDME on the graphite surface and in the graphite interlayer are 1.6 and 2.9 eV, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14611&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The fast Na-ion storage capability and high capacitive contribution of the EEG electrode promises its application in Na-ion capacitors. Thus, the prototype Na-ion capacitors were assembled using above EEG as the anode and commercial activated carbon (AC) as the cathode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DEGDME electrolyte. During the charging process, the PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> ions in the electrolyte will be adsorbed/desorbed on the surface of the AC cathode, while the Na ions were adsorbed and intercalated in the EEG anode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). Then, both PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na ions will be released back into the electrolyte in the discharging process. The working voltage of the fabricated Na-ion capacitor device is set in the range of 1 and 4.3 V based on the individual working potentials of the anode and cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a>). The mass ratio of the anode material to the cathode material is fixed to be 1:1.85, according to the balance of total charges (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b exhibits the charge/discharge profiles of the fabricated Na-ion capacitors at various current rates. Because of the combination of faradaic and non-faradaic charge storage mechanisms, the discharge profiles of the Na-ion capacitors are nonlinear. Impressively, the Na-ion capacitors can complete an entire charge/discharge process in 8 s at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Compared with conventional Na-ion capacitors, the better fast-charging ability of this Na-ion capacitor was attributed to the highly conductive EEG anode materials with enhanced surface-controlled capacitive Na-ion storage. Notably, because this full-cell device operates based on the principle of a hybrid supercapacitor instead of an Na-ion battery, we calculate the specific capacitance (F g<sup>–1</sup>) instead of the specific capacity (mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). Based on the charging/discharging curves at different current densities, the obtained specific capacitances of the fabricated Na-ion capacitors were 44, 30, 24, 18, 13, and 11 F g<sup>–1</sup> at specific current densities of 1, 2, 3, 5, 8, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). The calculated energy and power densities of the as-assembled Na-ion capacitors at various current densities are shown in the Ragone plot in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. The energy density of this Na-ion capacitor can reach 90 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> at 2000 W kg<sup>–1</sup> based on the total mass of active anode and cathode materials. The energy density of our device is comparable to that of recently reported Na-ion capacitors, such as Zn<sub>0.25</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O@graphene//mesoporous carbon (89 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> N-doped carbon fibers//salt-templated carbon (95 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> TiO<sub>2</sub>@CNT//AC (55 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> and N-rich carbon@mesoporous carbon//salt-templated carbon (61 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> The maximum power density of this Na-ion capacitor is 17,100 W kg<sup>–1</sup> with a corresponding energy density of 17 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. The power density of our device exceeds many previously reported Na-ion capacitors based on disordered carbon materials and transition-metal oxide/sulfide/carbide anodes, such as Zn<sub>0.25</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O@graphene//mesoporous carbon (12,552 W kg<sup>–1</sup> at 13.2 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> disordered carbon//AC (3240 W kg<sup>–1</sup> at 12 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> MnO@hollow carbon//AC (4200 W kg<sup>–1</sup> at 13.2 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> Mo<sub>2</sub>C//AC (2240 W kg<sup>–1</sup> at 8 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>@graphene//AC (7200 W kg<sup>–1</sup> at 9.7 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> and WS<sub>2</sub>//hollow carbon (7840 W kg<sup>–1</sup> at 17 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> Anyway, the EEG anode has better electronic conductivity and higher capacitive contributions compared with the anodes based on disordered carbon materials and transition-metal oxide/sulfide/carbide anodes. Apart from the superior power output, another key innovation of our Na-ion capacitor device is the simplicity and potential for the low-cost synthesis of electrochemically exfoliated graphite compared with that of the state-of-the-art electrochemical energy storage devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57 ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 57 to 59">(57−59)</a> Even so, because of the lower tap density of carbon materials than transition-metal oxides/sulfides/carbides, the volumetric power densities of this Na-ion capacitor still cannot be competitive with the above Na-ion electrochemical energy storage devices based on transition-metal oxide/sulfide/carbide anodes. In addition, the fabricated Na-ion capacitor delivers 100% capacitance retention after 700 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f). In addition, 32 commercial red light-emitting diodes can be stably powered for over 5 min using the charged Na-ion capacitors (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/medium/am0c14611_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical properties of the assembled Na-ion capacitors based on the EEG anode. (a) Schematic illustration of the working principle of the Na-ion capacitor. (b, c) Discharge/charge curves at various current rates. (d) Corresponding specific capacitances at various current rates. (e) Ragone plot and its comparison with other electrochemical energy storage/conversion devices. (f) Cycle stability at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/asset/images/large/am0c14611_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c14611&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we demonstrate the viability of electrochemically exfoliated graphite anode materials for high-rate Na-ion hybrid capacitors. Kinetic analysis confirms a high capacitive contribution of ∼81% in the electrochemically exfoliated graphite anode even at a low sweep rate of 1.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The 2D ultrathin nanosheets can expose more active sites for capacitive charge storage, greatly improving the kinetics of the Na<sup>+</sup>-diglyme co-intercalation reaction. The fabricated Na-ion capacitors based on the EEG anode delivered a high energy density of 90 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> at 2000 W kg<sup>–1</sup> and a maximum power density of 17,100 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> at 17 kW kg<sup>–1</sup> with a capacitance retention of 100% after 700 cycles. This work suggests that proper structural engineering of graphite is highly promising for high-performance Na-ion energy storage devices, particularly for high-rate operations.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c14611?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c14611</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM, TEM, and HR-TEM images of the synthesized EEG nanosheets; Raman spectroscopy and N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of EEG nanosheets; galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles of pristine graphite and EEG electrodes for the initial, the second, and the third cycles; SEM images of the cross section of the EEG film for electrodes; CV curves of the EEG electrode at various sweep rates; and the CV curve of the AC cathode and EEG anode; and galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles of AC cathode at 0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c14611/suppl_file/am0c14611_si_001.pdf">am0c14611_si_001.pdf (1.24 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c14611" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00116"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paek, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitlin, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review of Hybrid Ion Capacitors: from Aqueous to Lithium to Sodium</span>. <i>Chem. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>118</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">6457</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6498</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00116</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D118%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D6457-6498%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26author%3DJ.%2BDing%26author%3DW.%2BHu%26author%3DE.%2BPaek%26author%3DD.%2BMitlin%26title%3DReview%2Bof%2BHybrid%2BIon%2BCapacitors%253A%2Bfrom%2BAqueous%2Bto%2BLithium%2Bto%2BSodium%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemrev.8b00116&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c14611&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemrev.8b00116&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review of Hybrid Ion Capacitors: From Aqueous to Lithium to Sodium</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ding, Jia; Hu, Wenbin; Paek, Eunsu; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">118</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6457-6498</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this crit. Review we focus on the evolution of the hybrid ion capacitor (HIC) from its early embodiments to its modern form, focusing on the key outstanding scientific and technol. questions that necessitate further in-depth study.  It may be argued that HICs began as aq. systems, based on a Faradaic oxide pos. electrode (e.g., Co3O4, RuOx) and an activated carbon ion-adsorption neg. electrode.  In these early embodiments HICs were meant to compete directly with elec. double layer capacitors (EDLCs), rather than with the much higher energy secondary batteries.  The HIC design then evolved to be based on a wide voltage (∼4.2 V) carbonate-based battery electrolyte, using an insertion titanium oxide compd. anode (Li4Ti5O12, LixTi5O12) vs. a Li ion adsorption porous carbon cathode.  The modern Na and Li architectures contain a diverse range of nanostructured materials in both electrodes, including TiO2, Li7Ti5O12, Li4Ti5O12, Na6LiTi5O12, Na2Ti3O7, graphene, hard carbon, soft carbon, graphite, carbon nanosheets, pseudocapacitor T-Nb2O5, V2O5, MXene, conversion compds. MoS2, VN, MnO, and Fe2O3/Fe3O4, cathodes based on Na3V2(PO4)3, NaTi2(PO4)3, sodium super ionic conductor (NASICON), etc.  The Ragone chart characteristics of HIC devices critically depend on their anode-cathode architectures.  Combining electrodes with the flattest capacity vs. voltage characteristics, and the largest total voltage window, yields superior energy.  Unfortunately "flat voltage" materials undergo significant vol. expansion/contraction during cycling and are frequently lifetime limited.  Overall more research on HIC cathodes is needed; apart from high surface area carbon, very few pos. electrodes demonstrate the necessary 10 000 or 100 000 plus cycle life.  It remains to be detd. whether its lithium ion capacitors (LICs) or sodium ion capacitors (NICs) are superior in terms of energy-power and cyclability.  We discuss unresolved issues, including poorly understood fast-charge storage mechanisms, prelithiation and presodiation, solid electrolyte interface (SEI) formation, and high-rate metal plating.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrewdRK0N8NObVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhg1QnitBsEBQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXht1Sqtb7P&amp;md5=7d4cc9accdc9edb9cb6ec78504bf3620</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C6EE02070D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nanostructured Positive Electrode Materials for Post-Lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanostructured positive electrode materials for post-lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Faxing; Wu, Xiongwei; Li, Chunyang; Zhu, Yusong; Fu, Lijun; Wu, Yuping; Liu, Xiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3570-3611</span>CODEN:
